A</a></span>, 2024, <strong>12</strong>, 32794-32805</div><h1 id="sect213"><span class="title_heading">An improved and scalable method for the preparation of Sn-doped hexagonal tungsten bronze-type iron fluoride materials as cathodes for sodium-ion batteries<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a></span></h1><p class="header_text">

    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      10th May 2024
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 22nd July 2024</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 23rd July 2024</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>With their potentially high energy density and high safety, FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> materials are viewed as very promising candidate cathode materials for the next generation of lightweight SIBs (sodium-ion batteries). However, so far, the fabrication procedures for FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> materials have commonly entailed high-energy ball milling, solvothermal synthesis, utilization of costly ionic liquids, and even handling highly hazardous gases, rendering the preparation process intricate and impeding the successful incorporation of heteroatom doping into FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> materials. Herein, an improved and scalable aqueous solution approach is developed and implemented to obtain FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (hexagonal tungsten bronze-type) materials. Currently, in the field of improving the cycling performance of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> materials through metal doping, research focus has been primarily on transition metal doping, with limited investigation into non-transition metal doping. In non-transition metals, due to the similar electronegativity of Sn and Fe and the low cost of Sn, the electrochemical characteristics of Sn-doped FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O were investigated in this work. Meanwhile, the utilization of DFT reveals that Sn doping increases the atomic cloud density at neighboring Fe sites and expands the unit cell, consequently enhancing the conductivity and ion diffusion rate of the material. The synthesis method is simple and practical, providing a reliable experimental approach for promoting the commercialization of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O.</p></div><hr>
    
      <h2 id="sect311"><span class="a_heading">Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>As the world transitions towards renewable energy sources, the need for reliable and cost-effective energy storage systems becomes paramount. SIBs offer several advantages such as abundant raw material availability, lower cost, and reduced environmental impact, which have sparked significant research interest in developing SIBs as a viable energy storage technology. In SIBs, the cathode materials have a significant impact on their cost and energy density.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1</span></sup></a> Transition metal fluorides, which undergomulti-electron conversion reactions, have high theoretical specific capacity and high work voltage plateaus.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2,3</span></sup></a> Among them, due to the low cost, abundant resources of iron element and environmental friendliness, FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> materials are receiving significant attention from researchers.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">In general, the synthesis methods of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> materials can be categorized into three types: gas-phase synthesis, solid-phase synthesis and liquid-phase synthesis.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4</span></sup></a> In the gas-phase synthesis approach, FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> materials are prepared by a direct reaction between a precursor containing an iron source and a fluorine-containing gas (<span class="italic">e.g.</span>, F<small><sub>2</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5</span></sup></a> NF<small><sub>3</sub></small> (ref. <a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit6">6</a>) and HF<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2,7</span></sup></a>) at a designated temperature. This method carries significant production safety risks due to the high toxicity of the precursors involved. Solid-phase synthesis involves directly sintering the precursor of the iron source and fluorine source (such as NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>F,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8</span></sup></a> polytetrafluoroethylene,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9</span></sup></a> and CF<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a>) at high temperatures to prepare FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>. High-energy ball milling is a commonly employed solid-phase route to reduce the particle size of electrode materials and enhance their electrochemical performance as cathodes for SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2,11–13</span></sup></a> In liquid-phase synthesis, the soluble iron source and fluoride source (such as HF solution,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14</span></sup></a> NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>HF<small><sub>2</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15</span></sup></a> H<small><sub>2</sub></small>SiF<small><sub>6</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16</span></sup></a> F-containing ionic liquids,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17–19</span></sup></a><span class="italic">etc.</span>) are directly mixed and subjected to a solvothermal reaction. The resulting solution or suspension should be subjected to additional processing steps, such as filtration or drying, to yield the desired FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> materials. Liquid-phase synthesis offers inherent advantages, including adjustable reaction parameters and the ability to tailor the morphology and properties of the materials. However, due to high aqueous solubility, especially in HF solution, the yield of precipitated FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> in the solvothermal process is low. As a common practice, organic solvents or ionic liquids are often employed as alternative solvents to enhance the yield.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17,20–22</span></sup></a> Thus, the improvement of synthesis methods based on aqueous solutions is highly urgent. Moreover, FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> suffers from low intrinsic conductivity, incomplete conversion reactions, large voltage hysteresis and low electrochemical activity. Numerous strategies have been developed to solve these problems, including the combination of conductive carbon materials, surface modification, beneficial cation doping, and the exploration of new hydrated FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> species.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4,17,23,24</span></sup></a> Special attention is paid to the fact that the strategy of combining conductive carbon materials is necessary, as the highly insulating FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> materials otherwise exhibit poor reversible capacity and rapidly decaying cycling performance, thereby limiting their practical application in SIBs. Under the premise of constructing a carbon conductive network, researchers have improved their electrochemical properties by doping with various transition metals (Mn,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2</span></sup></a> Ni,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22</span></sup></a> Co,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25,26</span></sup></a> Ti,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11,12</span></sup></a> Cu,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a> Nb,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28</span></sup></a><span class="italic">etc.</span>). However, to the best of our knowledge, research on doping non-transition metals into FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> materials is still lacking.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Based on these research findings, under the premise of constructing graphene conductive pathways <span class="italic">in situ</span>, we selected Sn, a non-transition metal with similar electronegativity to Fe and cost advantages, and investigated the influence of different Sn-doping levels on the electrochemical performance of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O materials in SIBs. In this study, a comprehensive analysis of the material preparation process was conducted, revealing its high scalability potential. The key innovation of this method lies directly dissolving iron powder into a hydrofluoric acid solution under strongly oxidative conditions generated using H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, resulting in the formation of a colourless and stable complex ion group (FeF<small><sub>6</sub></small><small><sup>3−</sup></small>), which avoids the pre-preparation process of the iron source precursor and the post-treatment process of ball-milling technology. Subsequently, a series of Sn<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO (<span class="italic">x</span> = 0, 0.04, 0.06, 0.08, 0.10) cathode materials for SIBs were prepared using the aforementioned method. The results revealed that Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> (Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO) exhibited excellent cycling reversible capacity (115.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 100 cycles at 20 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) and fabulous cycling stability (a capacity retention of 82% after 100 cycles at 20 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>). Meanwhile, theoretical calculations were conducted using density functional theory (DFT) based on first principles (physics), which revealed that the introduction of Sn alters the electron cloud density at certain iron sites within the crystal and expands the unit cell, leading to an enhancement in the conductivity of the material and the ion diffusion rate of the material.</p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect382"><span class="a_heading">Experimental section</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect384"><span class="b_heading">Materials</span></h3>
        <span>All materials were purchased from Sigma-Aldrich and were untreated, including iron powder (reduced, AR ≥ 99%), tin powder (AR ≥ 99.5%), hydrofluoric acid (wt% ≥ 40.0%), hydrogen peroxide (wt% ≥ 30.0%), zinc oxide (AR ≥ 99%), titanium foil (AR ≥ 99%), nickel foam (AR ≥ 99%) and sodium chloride (AR ≥ 99%). Graphene oxide (GO) was prepared by a modified Hummers' method and dried graphene oxide was obtained through freeze-drying.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">The GO preparation process has been described in detail in our previous work.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit29"><sup><span class="sup_ref">29</span></sup></a> Briefly, after graphite was oxidized and turned bright yellow upon the addition of H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the mixture was naturally coagulated with 5−10% HCl solution and washed with deionized water several times until the pH &gt; 2. Then the GO solution (5 mg mL<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) was dispersed <span class="italic">via</span> ultrasonic-assisted dispersion for 30 min until the appearance of the liquid crystal phenomenon. Subsequently, the optimized amount of lauramide propyl amine oxide was added slowly with the assistance of mechanical agitation, until all GO sheets separated from the aqueous solution and the solution became transparent. The precipitate was purified by repeatedly dispersion it in water filtration through a 0.2 μm CA filter membrane. Eventually, the filtered product was freeze-dried at −35 °C and 6–8 Pa for 12 h to remove the residual water for subsequent processing.</p>
        <div>
          <span id="sect393"><span class="c_heading_indent">Synthesis of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO and Me<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO. </span>
          <span>
            <a title="Select to navigate to scheme" href="#imgsch1">Scheme 1</a> displays a schematic diagram of the synthesis process of Me<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO (Me = metal, such as Sn, Zn, Ti, and Ni) composites. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to scheme" href="#imgsch1">Scheme 1</a>, the fabrication process was performed using plastic beakers. First, 6 mL of concentrated HF acid was transferred to a plastic beaker and 2 g of pre-prepared iron powder and other metal powders or metal oxide powders were added while stirring. Their stoichiometric ratios are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>. When the brown color of the solution is no longer dark, H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> is added drop by drop until the solution becomes clear and transparent, where the volume ratio of HF and H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> is 3<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">10. Then, 0.4 g GO is added to the aforementioned clear and transparent solution and stirred for 30 min, resulting in a dark gray solution. Note that the above process is completed in a fume hood. It should be noted that the stirring operation should not be replaced by ultrasonic treatment, as yellow iron fluoride crystals will be partially precipitated. After stirring for 30 min, the obtained dark gray solution is transferred to the cold trap of a freeze vacuum dryer at −60 °C and frozen for 2 h. Then, it is freeze-vacuum dried for 24 h to obtain FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO or Me<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO (0 &lt; <span class="italic">x</span> ≤ 0.1). Finally, FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO or Me<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO was placed in a tube furnace with an argon flow (40 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> min<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) and sintered at 220 °C for 6 h to obtain the final product FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO or Me<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO.</span>
          <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgsch1"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-s1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03267e-s1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-s1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Scheme 1 </b> <span id="sch1"><span class="graphic_title">Schematic representation of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO or Me<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO composite material preparation.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
          
<div class="table_caption"><b>Table 1</b> <span id="tab1">Detailed table of synthesis for Me<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO materials and their abbreviation</span>
		</div>

              </table></div></div>
            <hr class="hrule"><br>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          <span id="sect543"><span class="c_heading_indent">Material characterization. </span>
          <span>X-ray diffraction (XRD, Rigaku-TTRIII), Japan, (Cu Kα radiation, scan rate of 10° min<small><sup>−1</sup></small>), transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEM-2100F, Japan) and field emission scanning electron microscopy (SEM, JSM-7900F, Japan) were used to test the morphology and structure of all samples. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis was performed using an ESCA-3400 instrument with monochromatic Cu Kα radiation. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, SDT Q600, New Castle, DE, USA) was performed in Ar, at a heating rate of 10 °C min<small><sup>−1</sup></small>.</span>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          <span id="sect548"><span class="c_heading_indent">Electrochemical characterization. </span>
          <span>The prepared samples, conductive carbon black and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) with a mass ratio of 7<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">2<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1 were ground in <span class="italic">N</span>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) to form a homogeneous slurry. Then the slurry was coated on the surface of aluminum foils and vacuum dried for 6 h at 100 °C. After that, the foils were punched into wafers with a radius of 6 mm for cell assembly. The areal density of the cathode material was 2–4 mg cm<small><sup>−2</sup></small>. Meanwhile, CR2025 cells were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox using the as-prepared electrode wafer with glass fiber (Whatman GF/D) as the separator, a Na metal foil as the counter electrode, and 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) solution (volume ratio 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1) with 5.0% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as the electrolyte.</span>
          <p class="otherpara">A NEWARE BTS Client battery system was employed to test cycle performance from 1.2 to 4.2 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na) at various current densities. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were conducted on a CHI760E electrochemical workstation in the same voltage range at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. After the button cell completed 4 CV cycles, the CHI760E electrochemical workstation was used to perform electrochemical impedance spectroscopy in the frequency range from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz with an alternating current voltage amplitude of 5 mV at the open-circuit voltage. For the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) test, the cells were charged at 10 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (0.1C) for 3 min and then allowed to relax for 30 min.</p>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          <span id="sect559"><span class="c_heading_indent">Calculation method. </span>
          <span>All computational simulations were performed using Vienna <span class="italic">Ab Initio</span> Simulation Package (VASP) software based on density functional theory (DFT).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30,31</span></sup></a> The projector-augmented-wave (PAW) technique and Perdew−Burke−Ernzerhof (PBE) generalized gradient approximation (GGA) method were employed to address the core-electron interaction and exchange–correlation potential, respectively, when processing.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32–34</span></sup></a> The unit cell of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>, similar to the ReO<small><sub>3</sub></small> structure with the space group <span class="italic">Fd</span>3<span class="italic">m</span>, consists of 64 atoms (16 Fe and 48 F). Likewise, the unit cell of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, resembling the hexagonal tungsten bronze (HTB) structure with the space group <span class="italic">Cmcm</span>, contains 60 atoms (12 Fe, 36 F, 4 O, and 8 H), while the unit cell of Sn-doped HTB-FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O) comprises 60 atoms (1 Sn, 11 Fe, 36 F, 4 O, and 8 H). The PBE pseudopotentials used for the calculations have atomic valence layer configurations of 3d<small><sup>7</sup></small>4s<small><sup>1</sup></small>, 4d<small><sup>10</sup></small>5s<small><sup>2</sup></small>5p<small><sup>2</sup></small>, 2s<small><sup>2</sup></small>2p<small><sup>5</sup></small>, 2s<small><sup>2</sup></small>2p<small><sup>4</sup></small>, and 1s<small><sup>1</sup></small> for Fe, Sn, F, O, and H. Since not all of the d-electron orbitals in the element Fe are in the occupied state, a correction was made using the GGA + <span class="italic">U</span> method,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> and the effective parameters chosen, <span class="italic">U</span> and <span class="italic">J</span>, were 6.8 and 0.89 eV.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36,37</span></sup></a> In the PAW basis set, a kinetic energy cut-off of 520 eV is chosen to determine its span in reciprocal space. For the unit cell of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O and Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, the Monkhorst–Pack scheme is applied with a 9 × 5 × 9 <span class="italic">k</span>-point mesh to sample the Brillouin zone.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a> Similarly, for the unit cell of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>, the Brillouin zone is sampled using the Monkhorst–Pack scheme with a 9 × 9 × 9 <span class="italic">k</span>-point mesh. The convergence criterion for ionic relaxation is set to 0.1 meV per cell, while the convergence criterion for self-consistent field (SCF) iterations of electronic calculations is set to 0.01 meV per cell. In addition, the behavior of water in this calculation is accurately described using the optB88-VDW calculation method in VASP.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39,40</span></sup></a> Spin polarization calculations were implemented in all calculations.</span>
        </span></div>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect604"><span class="a_heading">Results and discussion</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect606"><span class="b_heading">Analysis of the synthetic mechanism and characterization of the structure and morphology</span></h3>
        <span>
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a> illustrates the evolution of matter during material synthesis. Herein, in the course of the experiment, it was difficult to completely dissolve monomorphic iron into hydrofluoric acid solution. After a certain amount of H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> solution was added, the solution faded from iron-brown to colourless, indicating complete dissolution. The reason is that in a strong oxidizing environment, Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> can easily form a strong complex ion group (FeF<small><sub>6</sub></small><small><sup>3−</sup></small>) with fluoride ions, which makes the iron dissolve quickly. In contrast, in a weak oxidizing environment, iron cannot dissolve completely and forms a brown cloudy solution containing Fe<small><sup>2+</sup></small>, FeF<small><sub>6</sub></small><small><sup>4−</sup></small>, Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> and undissolved metallic iron. The chemical mechanisms of the reactions are shown in the following chemical equations, respectively:<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><span id="eqn1">Fe<small><sup>2+</sup></small> + 6F<small><sup>−</sup></small> <img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_21cb.gif" alt="[left over right harpoons]"> FeF<small><sub>6</sub></small><small><sup>4−</sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2"><span id="eqn2">Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> + 6F<small><sup>−</sup></small> ⇒ FeF<small><sub>6</sub></small><small><sup>3−</sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table></span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03267e-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The evolution of matter during material synthesis. (b) TG curve of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O recorded from 20 to 800 °C at a heating rate of 10 °C min<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in an Ar atmosphere. (c) Structural evolution before and after 8% Sn modification obtained by computational simulation. (d) XRD patterns of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small>, including enlarged details of the 220 and 002 crystal planes of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O and the 110 crystal plane of (SnF)<small><sub>2</sub></small>SnF<small><sub>6</sub></small>. (e) Classification of Fe sites in crystals into S<small><sub>1</sub></small> and S<small><sub>2</sub></small> positions based on Bader charge analysis. (f) XPS spectra in the region of the Fe 2p peak of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small>, respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The chemical equilibrium constant for <a title="" href="#eqn2">eqn (2)</a> is <span class="italic">K</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> = 10<small><sup>15.04</sup></small>, which means that in a strongly oxidizing environment iron ions are almost always present as FeF<small><sub>6</sub></small><small><sup>3−</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20,41</span></sup></a> To further verify that highly stable FeF<small><sub>6</sub></small><small><sup>3−</sup></small> was produced, saturated saline was added to the solution from <a title="Select to navigate to scheme" href="#imgsch1">Scheme 1</a>, which instantly produced a yellowish precipitate, the XRD pattern of which (Fig. S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) indicates that the structure is Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>FeF<small><sub>6</sub></small>. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a>, the substance obtained after cryodesiccation is FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (Fig. S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). To verify the scalability of this doping method, in addition to the doping of Sn, the doping of Zn, Ti, and Ni was also attempted. Fig. S3–S5<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> sequentially reveal the synthesis, structure and component information of the samples doped with different elements. With the addition of H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Fig. S3b<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), the turbid and brown solution gradually became transparent and clarified, forming a stable and colourless group of FeF<small><sub>6</sub></small><small><sup>3−</sup></small> ions. The structural information was explored using XRD tests (Fig. S4<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), and the positions of the diffraction peaks were found to correspond roughly to those of the standard PDF no. 76-1262 card,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2</span></sup></a> which indicates the feasibility of the preparation method of this doped material. Further, the morphology of all the materials prepared by this method consisted of nanometre (nm)-sized spherical particles (Fig. S5<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). Due to the absence of graphene, these primary nanometre (nm)-sized particles agglomerated to form micrometre (μm)-sized secondary particles. Finally, EDS mapping analysis shows (Fig. S5<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) that there is no segregation phenomenon in the distribution of elements at the micrometre scale, further indicating the uniformity and tunable properties of the preparation method for this doped material.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a> shows the TG (thermogravimetric) curve of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, which demonstrated three weightless stages according to the relative molecular weights of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (HTB, hexagonal tungsten bronze) and FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>(HTB). According to the above stages, FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O was heated up to 60 °C, 220 °C, and 600 °C in turn and held for 1 h. Samples S60, S220, and S800 were obtained and the corresponding XRD patterns are shown in Fig. S6.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> S60 is still the FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O phase, and S220 is FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (HTB). However, S600 is a mixture of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>, FeF<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small>F<small><sub>5</sub></small>. Interestingly, it was found that the water in FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (HTB) belongs to zeolite water,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a> indicating that water molecules are lost at low temperatures, while the octahedral structure remains unchanged during the conversion from FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (HTB) to FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> (HTB), as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a>. Moreover, at relatively high temperature (above 254 °C), FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> (HTB) is irreversibly converted to FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> (ReO<small><sub>3</sub></small>),<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a> followed by a gradual transformation of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> (ReO<small><sub>3</sub></small>) to FeF<small><sub>2</sub></small> (TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>),<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14</span></sup></a> which reveals the importance of controlling temperature in the preparation process of the materials. Using this method to prepare FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (HTB) materials avoids the issue of low material yield caused by the dissolution of iron fluoride materials in solvents such as HF solution. Theoretically, when calculated based on iron element loss, the yield of this method can reach 100%. Despite some transfer losses during actual laboratory operations, the practical yield still exceeds 95%.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a>, when one Fe site in the crystal lattice of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O is replaced by one Sn atom, the Sn-doping concentration is 8.3%, which indicates that Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> is closest to the ideal crystal structure. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a> shows structural evolution before and after 8% Sn modification obtained by computational simulation, resulting in the enlargement of the crystal cell (expansion of the value of <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>220</sub></small> from 3.19 to 3.28 Å). In Fig. S3d,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> show significantly different colours as the Sn-doping concentration increases, with the colour gradually changing from green to light yellow.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Fig. S7<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> shows the XRD patterns generated by simulation using the DFT calculation model of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O from <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a>. Compared to the standard PDF no.76-1262 card, the positions of the diffraction peaks are consistent, indicating the accuracy of the DFT calculation model. The inset of Fig. S7<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> shows the simulated diffraction peak of Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O from <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a>, which is left-shifted with respect to the simulated diffraction peak of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O. According to Bragg's equation (2<span class="italic">d</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">sin<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]"><span class="italic">θ</span> = <span class="italic">nλ</span>), the leftward shift in the value of 2<span class="italic">θ</span> implies an increase in the interplanar spacing.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a> shows the XRD patterns of all samples (Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small>), matching well with the standard peaks of PDF no.76-1262. The diffraction peaks of 220 and 002 from FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O shift slightly to smaller angles as the concentration of doped Sn increases, which is attributed to the fact that the radius of the Sn atom is larger than that of the Fe atom. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a>, a pronounced diffraction peak of 110 (the strongest diffraction peak of the (SnF)<small><sub>2</sub></small>SnF<small><sub>6</sub></small> standard card in Fig. S7<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) appeared in the Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> sample, which indicated that excess Sn formed tin fluoride.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1e</a>, the Fe sites in the crystal are divided into <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> and <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> positions based on Bader charge analysis. Herein, <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> positions stand for the high charge number Fe (2.05), while <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> positions represent the low charge number Fe (2.04, 2.03, and 1.55). In the XPS (X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy) test, the area ratio of <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>/<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> can measure the charge number of Fe in the material system; a higher <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>/<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> value indicates a higher proportion of Fe with a high charge number, while a smaller <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>/<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> value indicates a higher proportion of Fe with a low charge number. In order to investigate the chemical environment of each element in the material, XPS testing was performed. The XPS spectra of Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small>, and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> are shown in Fig. S8,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> exhibiting the characteristic peaks of C 1s, Sn 3d, O 1s, F 1s, and Fe 2p at approximately 284 eV, 487 eV, 532 eV, 684 eV, and 713 eV, respectively. The Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> sample, which does not contain doped Sn, did not show the Sn 3d feature peak at approximately 487 eV. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1f</a> shows the XPS spectra of Fe 2p for Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small>, respectively, which usually consist of two sub-diffraction peaks, Fe 2p<small><sup>3/2</sup></small> and Fe 2p<small><sup>1/2</sup></small>, accompanied by two satellite peaks in turn (Fe 2p<small><sup>3/2</sup></small> satellite and Fe 2p<small><sup>1/2</sup></small> satellite). In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1f</a>, the XPS spectrum of Fe 2p<small><sup>3/2</sup></small> is positioned above 710 eV, which indicates that Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> is the dominant oxidation state.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8,13,22,42–46</span></sup></a> Combined with the Bader charge analysis of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1f</a>, the XPS spectrum of Fe2p<small><sup>3/2</sup></small> is fitted with the two peaks of <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> (713.56 eV) and <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> (715.96 eV). In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1f</a>, the <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>/<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> values of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small>, and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small>, are 0.65, 0.60, 0.51, 0.46 and 0.31, respectively. As the Sn concentration increases, the value of <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>/<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> decreases, indicating a higher proportion of Fe with lower oxidation states. Additionally, due to the splitting of the Fe 2p spin–orbit orbitals, the peak area ratio of the chemical states corresponding to Fe 2p<small><sup>2/3</sup></small> and Fe 2p<small><sup>1/2</sup></small> orbitals is 2<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1, and this principle was considered during the fitting process (<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>1</sub></small>/<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub><span class="italic">a</span></sub></small> = <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>/<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub><span class="italic">b</span></sub></small> = 2). Meanwhile, Fig. S9<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> shows the XPS spectra of Sn 3d for Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small>, and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small>, with Sn 3d<small><sup>5/2</sup></small> and Sn 3d<small><sup>3/2</sup></small> peaks located at 468.73 eV and 495.13 eV, respectively, indicating that Sn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> is the main oxidation state due to the introduction of H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47–50</span></sup></a></p>
        <p class="otherpara">The morphology of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> (FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO) and Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> (Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/GO) was characterized by SEM and TEM, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2</a>. The SEM image shows that the nanometre (nm)-sized spherical active materials are uniformly distributed on graphene nanosheets, and the unique spherical morphology may be attributed to the surface tension of water during the low-temperature freeze-drying process. In the TEM image, enlarged local details of the nanospheres revealed that they were composed of numerous microcrystalline particles with a diameter of approximately 50 nm stacked together. HRTEM images showed the polycrystalline nature, exhibiting various crystal planes (220, 202, 224, <span class="italic">etc.</span>). SAED (selected area electron diffraction) signal collection was performed on the microcrystalline particles to obtain detailed structural information. The diffraction pattern of Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> exhibited distinct polycrystalline diffraction rings. However, the diffraction pattern of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> not only contained diffraction rings but also included more regular diffraction spots, indicating that Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> led to a more disordered crystal face orientation in the material. Additionally, the interplanar spacing was calculated from the diffraction spots. The interplanar spacing of various crystal faces in Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> (<span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>220</sub></small> = 0.36 nm, <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>202</sub></small> = 0.29 nm, and <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>224</sub></small> = 0.19 nm) was larger than that in Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> (<span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>220</sub></small> = 0.32 nm, <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>202</sub></small> = 0.26 nm, and <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>224</sub></small> = 0.16 nm). EDX (energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy) was used to confirm the composition of the particles. EDX mapping images demonstrate that the nanocrystals of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> were mainly composed of evenly distributed F, Fe, and O elements, which are prominent features of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O nanoparticles. Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> exhibited a uniform distribution of Sn within the FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O particles, indicating that Sn doping occurred in the FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O crystal.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03267e-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a) SEM image, TEM image, EDX mapping images, HRTEM image and SAED pattern of Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small>. (b) SEM image, TEM image, EDX mapping images, HRTEM image and SAED pattern of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect891"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical performance analysis</span></h3>
        <span>A series of electrochemical performance tests were conducted on sodium-ion half-cells, where cathode electrodes were assembled using Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small>, and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> samples, to explore the effects of Sn-doped FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O on their sodium storage capacity and cycling stability. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a> showcases the cycling performance of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small>, and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> at a low current density of 20 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The analysis of the data in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a> reveals that, during the initial charging process, Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small>, and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> exhibit specific capacities of 194.0, 160.0, 148.9, 141.0, and 101.7 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. It demonstrates a decreasing trend in capacity as the Sn-doping concentration increases, indicating that the Sn incorporated into the crystal lattice may not contribute to the capacity. Furthermore, in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a>, the capacities of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, and Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small> consistently decrease with an increase in the number of cycles, especially with Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> showing the most significant decay. In contrast, except for the initial few cycles, the capacities of Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> show less apparent attenuation with an increase in the number of cycles. After 100 cycles, the reversible specific capacity of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> has decreased to 93.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (a capacity retention of 49%), while the reversible specific capacity of Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> remains at 115.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (a capacity retention of 82%). It indicates that Sn doping can enhance the cycling stability of the material.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03267e-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The cycling performance of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> at a current density of 20 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the range of 1.2–4.2 V. (b) The charge−discharge voltage profiles of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> from the 1st cycle at a current density of 20 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the range of 1.2–4.2 V as well as ICE (inset). (c) Rate capability of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> at varying rates from 20 to 1000 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the range of 1.2–4.2 V. (d) The long-term cycling performance of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> at a large current density of 100 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the range of 1.2–4.2 V. (e) The cyclic voltammetry curves of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> for the initial four cycles at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. (f) EIS analysis of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> as well as the equivalent circuit model of EIS (inset). (g) Relationship between Z′ and <span class="italic">ω</span><small><sup>−1/2</sup></small> in the low-frequency region of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The first charge–discharge voltage profiles of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small>, and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> at a current density of 20 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and embedded data of the initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>. Except for Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small>, the ICE of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, and Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> increases sequentially, indicating that Sn doping contributes to the improvement of material reversibility. Based on the crystal model analysis, it is known that an Sn-doping concentration of 8% is closest to the ideal crystal structure with one heteroatom replacement (8.3%). Iron fluoride (an ICE of 81.4% at Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small>) exhibits significant irreversible capacity during the initial charge–discharge cycles, likely attributed to intense phase transformations occurring at lower voltages during the first conversion reaction, leading to partial deactivation of active materials. Additionally, despite sintering at 220 °C for 6 hours during material preparation to remove crystalline water completely and retain the HTB-type framework, trace amounts of residual water remain, thereby reducing the initial coulombic efficiency of the material. In contrast, the ICE of Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> was significantly reduced when the Sn-doping concentration exceeded 8%, In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a>, a pronounced diffraction peak of 110 (the strongest diffraction peak of the (SnF)<small><sub>2</sub></small>SnF<small><sub>6</sub></small> standard card in Fig. S7<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) appeared in the Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> sample, which indicated that excess Sn formed tin fluoride. The XPS spectrum (Fig. S9<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) and Bader charge analysis (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5i</a>) reveal that the Sn in the material exists as Sn<small><sup>4+</sup></small>.The step where Sn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> gains electrons to form Sn<small><sup>2+</sup></small> is considered irreversible.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47</span></sup></a> For Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>), during the initial discharge process, additional tin fluorides gain electrons and contribute to the capacity, but they are unable to provide the corresponding charge capacity during the first charge process, exacerbating the irreversibility of the material's conversion reaction at low voltages. These irreversible tin fluorides lose their activity after the first discharge, preventing the formation of HTB-type frameworks again at low voltages, making the migration path of sodium ions more tortuous and ultimately reducing the rate of sodium ion migration in the material. In summary, excess tin is present in the form of tin fluoride, which is categorized as an anode material for SIBs and exhibits irreversibility in the high voltage range,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47</span></sup></a> thereby greatly reducing the reversibility of the first cycle of Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small>.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>, two voltage plateaus appear in the initial discharge curve, at approximately 2.8 V and 1.5 V, corresponding to the following two reaction processes, respectively:<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2,20</span></sup></a></p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn3"><span id="eqn3">FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>(HTB) + Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + e<small><sup>−</sup></small> <img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_21cb.gif" alt="[left over right harpoons]"> NaFeF<small><sub>3</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(3)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn4"><span id="eqn4">FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>(HTB) + 2Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + 2e<small><sup>−</sup></small> <img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_21cb.gif" alt="[left over right harpoons]"> 3NaF + Fe</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(4)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">To further investigate the battery reaction mechanism, cyclic voltammetry was performed for the Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> button batteries, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>. The mechanism of energy storage in the FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> conversion reaction was proposed and experimentally demonstrated by Hua <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit51"><sup><span class="sup_ref">51</span></sup></a> Drawing on the research results of Hua, the conversion process of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O can be considered to consist of the following three stages:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn5"><span id="eqn5"><span class="italic">x</span>Na + (1 + 2<span class="italic">δ</span>)FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>(HTB) <img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_21cb.gif" alt="[left over right harpoons]"> A-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">δ</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small> + 3<span class="italic">δ</span>FeF<small><sub>2</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(5)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn6"><span id="eqn6">(1 + 2<span class="italic">δ</span> − <span class="italic">x</span>)Na + A-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">δ</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small> <img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_21cb.gif" alt="[left over right harpoons]"> B-Na<small><sub>1+2<span class="italic">δ</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">δ</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(6)</td></tr></tbody></table><br><table><tbody><tr><td class="eqnText">2(1 − <span class="italic">η</span>)Na + B-Na<small><sub>1+2<span class="italic">δ</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">δ</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small> <img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_21cb.gif" alt="[left over right harpoons]"> C-Na<small><sub>1+2<span class="italic">η</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">η</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small> + (<span class="italic">η</span> − <span class="italic">δ</span>)Fe</td></tr></tbody></table>and<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn7"><span id="eqn7">2(1 − <span class="italic">η</span>)Na + C-Na<small><sub>1+2<span class="italic">η</span></sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">η</span></sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small> <img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_21cb.gif" alt="[left over right harpoons]"> 3NaF + (1 − <span class="italic">η</span>)Fe</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(7)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">The reactions occurring in the region centered around peaks I and II in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a> correspond to <a title="" href="#eqn5">eqn (5)</a> and <a title="" href="#eqn6">(6)</a> respectively, while region III below 1.57 V corresponds to <a title="" href="#eqn7">eqn (7)</a>. The difference between the voltages of the oxidation (I<small><sub>o</sub></small> or II<small><sub>o</sub></small>) and reduction (I<small><sub>r</sub></small>, or II<small><sub>r</sub></small>) peaks measures the electrochemical reversibility; the smaller the difference, the better the reversibility, and <span class="italic">vice versa</span>. The differences of I<small><sub>o</sub></small>–I<small><sub>r</sub></small> (0.27 V) and II<small><sub>o</sub></small>–II<small><sub>r</sub></small> (0.57 V) for Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> are smaller than those of I<small><sub>o</sub></small>–I<small><sub>r</sub></small> (0.62 V) and II<small><sub>o</sub></small>–II<small><sub>r</sub></small> (0.83 V) for Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, respectively, suggesting that Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> has better reversibility for use in SIBs, probably thanks to the improved conductivity of the material. In addition, cyclic voltammetry curves for Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> are shown in Fig. S10.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> In Fig. S10,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> exhibits a stronger irreversible reduction peak during the first discharge, below 1.57 V, relative to the other samples. This is attributed to the irreversible tin fluoride providing additional capacity, leading to lower ICE, which also indirectly proves the existence of additional tin fluoride.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The rate performances of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>6</sub></small>, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>10</sub></small> at various current densities (20–1000 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) with each rate for 10 cycles are presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a> to further understand the influence of Sn doping on the rate performance. The capacity of all samples gradually decreased with increasing current density. Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> displays capacities of 128.1, 104.9, 82.0, 65.5, 50.2 and 33.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 20, 40, 100, 200, 400 and 1000 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>, by contrast, shows capacities of 152.0, 113.0, 77.2, 56.7, 39.8 and 24.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 20–1000 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. Although the initial capacity of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> is higher than that of Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small>, the capacity of Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> exceeds that of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> with the increase of the number of cycles and current density (after 30 cycles at 100 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>), and it is obvious that Sn doping enhances the rate performance of the material. To further evaluate the cycling stability of the electrode, the cycling performances of all samples at a large current density (1000 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) are illustrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a>. The capacities of Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> are 63.7 and 49.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 300 cycles, respectively. Remarkably, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> exhibits better cycling stability than Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small>.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3f</a>, to explain the intrinsic effect of Sn doping on the electrochemical properties of the material, EIS (Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy) of all samples was performed. To ensure test consistency, the button cell underwent four uniform cycles for EIS measurements. All samples have similar characteristics, including a semicircular charging transfer resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>) in the high-frequency region, representing the reaction kinetics of the electrode reaction, and a diffusion impedance (<span class="italic">Z</span><small><sub>W</sub></small>) with a quasilinear slope in the low-frequency region, related to the diffusion of ions in the electrode material. Nyquist spectra have been fitted and the corresponding equivalent circuit model is depicted in the inset of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3f</a>. The electrolyte resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small>) reflects the total impedance of the electrolyte and electrode material. From the fitted impedance parameters in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab2">Table 2</a>, it can be seen that the values of <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small> for Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> are smaller than those of the other samples, which is attributed to the suitable Sn-doping concentration of Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> to enhance the conductivity of the material.</p>

		</div>
