          <hr class="hrule"><br>
        <p class="otherpara">In addition, EIS data can be utilized to calculate the diffusion rate of sodium ions in the material (<span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small>), which can be calculated using the following equation (<a title="" href="#eqn8">eqn (8)</a>):</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn8"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-t1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03267e-t1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-t1.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(8)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">R</span> is the gas constant (8.314 J mol<small><sup>−1</sup></small> K<small><sup>−1</sup></small>), <span class="italic">T</span> represents the Kelvin temperature (298 K), <span class="italic">A</span> is the contact area between the electrode and the electrolyte (1.13 cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>), <span class="italic">n</span> represents the number of electrons transferred in the redox reaction (3.0), <span class="italic">F</span> is the Faraday constant (96<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">500C mol<small><sup>−1</sup></small>), <span class="italic">C</span> is the molar concentration of sodium ions in the electrolyte (1 × 10<small><sup>−3</sup></small> mol cm<small><sup>−3</sup></small>), and <span class="italic">σ</span> is the Warburg factor, which can be obtained from the linear relationship between <span class="italic">Z</span>′ and <span class="italic">ω</span><small><sup>−1/2</sup></small> as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3g</a>, where <span class="italic">ω</span> = 2π<span class="italic">f</span>. Ultimately, the calculated diffusion rate values of sodium ions (<span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small>) for all samples are listed in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab2">Table 2</a>. Apparently, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> has a faster diffusion rate of sodium ions compared to the other samples. Obviously, compared to the other samples, Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> (5.58 × 10<small><sup>−10</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) exhibits a faster diffusion rate of sodium ions, which indicates that the expansion of the unit cell caused by Sn doping is beneficial for enhancing the diffusion rate of sodium ions.<p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">The diffusion rates of sodium ions during charge–discharge processes were analyzed using GITT testing. The diffusion rates can be calculated using the following equation (<a title="" href="#eqn9">eqn (9)</a>):</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn9"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-t2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03267e-t2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-t2.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(9)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">The calculated parameters are summarized in Table S1.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4</a>, it was observed that the diffusion rates of sodium ions in Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> were consistently higher than those in Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> at almost all titration equilibrium voltages. The order of magnitude of the sodium ion migration rate values calculated from both the EIS test and the GITT test is around −10, which mutually corroborates the reliability of the data. It is noteworthy that, unlike the deintercalation reactions observed in layered oxides, iron fluoride materials undergo conversion reactions at low voltages. At the end of discharge and the beginning of charge, the migration rates of sodium ions differ by approximately 1000-fold, corresponding to the regeneration of iron fluoride frameworks, which hinders the migration of sodium ions.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03267e-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique (GITT) curves of Sn<small><sub>0</sub></small> and Sn<small><sub>8</sub></small> after 2 cycles within 1.2–4.2 V.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03267e-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">The model of the unit cell, deformation charge density or charge density difference, and Bader charge analysis of (a–c) FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>, (d–f) FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, and (g–i) Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect1273"><span class="b_heading">First-principles calculations</span></h3>
        <span>In 1983, researchers for the first time revealed that FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O crystallizes in the orthorhombic system with cell dimensions of <span class="italic">a</span> = 7.42 Å, <span class="italic">b</span> = 12.73 Å, and <span class="italic">c</span> = 7.52 Å, belonging to the space group <span class="italic">Cmcm</span>, and it was found that the framework of the FeF<small><sub>6</sub></small> octahedra is related to that of hexagonal tungsten bronze (HTB) Rb<small><sub>0.29</sub></small>WO<small><sub>3</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a> Based on the experimental data mentioned above, an initial crystal cell model of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O was constructed, and the structural optimization calculation was performed to obtain the lowest energy model as shown in Fig. S11d.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> Afterward, as shown in Fig. S11a–c,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> three different cases were considered in which one Sn atom was replaced on the Fe site, resulting in a doping concentration of 8.3% for the Sn element. In addition, the binding energies of the three doping models, labeled as Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-top, Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid, and Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-bot, are −194.903 eV, −194.942 eV, and −194.935 eV, respectively, while the binding energy of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O is −196.991 eV. This indicates that Sn doping leads to an increase in the system's energy. One of these three models, the Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid structure, shows the smallest energy increase. Therefore, when one Sn atom replaces an iron site, it is more likely to form the Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid structure. Compared to the cell size of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (<span class="italic">a</span> = 7.41 Å, <span class="italic">b</span> = 12.69 Å, and <span class="italic">c</span> = 7.46 Å), it is observed that the cell size after Sn doping has significantly increased, indicating that Sn doping leads to an enlargement of the hexagonal tunnels, which is beneficial for reducing the migration resistance of sodium ions within the unit cell.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a</a> shows the (111) crystal plane model of the unit cell of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> in the cubic system with the cell dimensions of <span class="italic">a</span> = <span class="italic">b</span> = <span class="italic">c</span> = 10.41 Å, belonging to the space group <span class="italic">Fd</span>3<span class="italic">m</span>. Additionally, <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5d and g</a> display the (001) crystal plane models of the unit cell of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O and the unit cell of Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid, respectively. To present the deformation charge density and Bader charge analysis more clearly, data plots were created specifically for the (111) or (001) crystal planes. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b and e</a> display the deformation charge density of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> and FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, respectively, calculated using the formula:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn10"><span id="eqn10">Δ<span class="italic">ρ</span> = <span class="italic">ρ</span>(ABC…) − <span class="italic">ρ</span>(A) − <span class="italic">ρ</span>(B) − <span class="italic">ρ</span>(C) − …</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(10)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">The expanded form of formula <a title="" href="#eqn10">(10)</a> in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b</a> is Δ<span class="italic">ρ</span> = <span class="italic">ρ</span>(FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>) − <span class="italic">ρ</span>(Fe) − <span class="italic">ρ</span>(F), while in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5e</a>, the expanded form is Δ<span class="italic">ρ</span> = <span class="italic">ρ</span>(FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O) − <span class="italic">ρ</span>(Fe) − <span class="italic">ρ</span>(F) − <span class="italic">ρ</span>(H) − <span class="italic">ρ</span>(O). The deformation charge density provides visualizations of properties such as charge transfer and bond polarization during electron coupling. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b and e</a>, the yellow region represents an increase in electron cloud density, while the lake blue region indicates a decrease in electron cloud density. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5h</a> represents the charge density difference of Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid relative to FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, calculated using the formula:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn11"><span id="eqn11">Δ<span class="italic">ρ</span> = <span class="italic">ρ</span>(AB) − <span class="italic">ρ</span>(AC)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(11)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">The expanded form of formula <a title="" href="#eqn11">(11)</a> in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5h</a> is Δ<span class="italic">ρ</span> = <span class="italic">ρ</span>(Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O) − ρ(FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O). In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5h</a>, it is worth noting that the calculation model for FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (AC) in this case involves directly replacing the Sn site from <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5g</a> with Fe, rather than using the model shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5d</a>. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5h</a>, it is evident that the introduction of Sn has a significant impact on the charge distribution of the horizontally bonded Fe atoms, which directly affects the conductivity of the material. To further quantify the electron transfer situation, Bader charge analysis was performed. From <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5f and i</a>, it can be seen that the introduction of Sn leads to a decrease in the charge number of the adjacent Fe atom (Fe<small><sub>3</sub></small> and Fe<small><sub>1</sub></small> 2.04|e| → 2.03|e|, Fe2 2.05|e| → 1.55|e|), which indicates that the Sn doping can weaken the strength of the F–Fe bond and is beneficial for improving the conductivity of the material.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The spin band structure and corresponding DOS (density of states) for the materials FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>, FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, and Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6a–c</a>, respectively. The calculation results indicate that the band gap of pure FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> (2.84 eV) is significantly larger than that of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (1.03 eV), which implies that, compared to pure FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>, the band structure of hydrated FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> is more favorable for electron transport. In addition, compared to FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (1.03 eV), Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid (0.98 eV) exhibits a slight reduction in the band gap, indicating that Sn doping can enhance the electronic conductivity of the material to some extent. Further analysis of the DOS of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> and FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6a and b</a>, reveals the presence of O 2p orbitals and a small number of H 1s orbitals near the Fermi level in FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, which reduce the bad gap from 2.84 eV for pure FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small> to 1.03 eV for the FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O structure.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03267e-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03267e/d4ta03267e-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">The spin band structure and DOS of (a) FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>, (b) FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O and (c) Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid, and (d) PDOS of Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Fe<small><sub>5</sub></small> sites in Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6b and c</a>, compared to FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid has a significantly increased number of valence electron bands between −2.0 eV and the Fermi level. Especially, in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6c</a> there is an additional spin-down Fe 3d band at 0.49 eV, indicating a noticeable change in the electron cloud of a specific iron site due to the introduction of Sn. It results in an increased number of valence electrons near the Fermi level, and these electrons have a higher probability of transitioning to the conduction band. Comprehensively analyzing <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6a–c</a>, it is found that the impurity energy band of zeolite water appears isolated in the forbidden band with a width of 2.84 eV, which greatly reduces the band gap but not the forbidden bandwidth, whereas the introduction of Sn directly reduces the width of the forbidden band from 2.84 eV to 2.54 eV, which is very beneficial for the enhancement of the electrical conductivity of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O. In order to further investigate the influence of Sn introduction on the band structure, Partial Density of States (PDOS) calculations were performed on Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Fe<small><sub>5</sub></small> (shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5i</a>) in Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O-mid, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6d</a>. The calculation indicates that the energy ranges from −1 eV to the Fermi level (0 eV) in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6c</a> and is solely contributed by the Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small> atom, which further confirms that Sn doping significantly alters the 3d orbital electronic configuration of Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small> atoms. In conclusion, the calculations using first-principles electronic structure calculations based on DFT indicate that Sn doping leads to an increase in electron cloud density in Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small>, resulting in a reduction of the band gap and an increase in the number of bands within the energy range from −1 eV to the Fermi level. It can be predicted that Sn doping will lead to a significant enhancement of the conductivity of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O.</p>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect1465"><span class="a_heading">Conclusions</span></h2>
      <span>In this work, we have provided a novel synthetic pathway for the FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O material. The generation of highly stable FeF<small><sub>6</sub></small><small><sup>3−</sup></small> complex ions in an aqueous solution was achieved by providing an oxidative environment (H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>). Subsequently, the precursor FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O was rapidly synthesized by freeze-drying, after which the final product FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O was obtained in high yield by dehydration at 220 °C. The advantage of this synthesis method, in addition to the direct synthesis in aqueous solution, is that it is very easy to adjust the doping of a variety of hetero-elements (such as Sn, Ti, Ni, Zn and so on) in the process of synthesis, providing a very simple experimental validation of the doping study of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O. Band structure and DOS calculations for the Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O material were performed using DFT based on first principles, revealing that the introduction of Sn improves the conductivity of the material. Meanwhile, a series of morphological and structural characterization techniques demonstrated that the crystal properties of Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O are consistent with theoretical calculations. The electrochemical performance of Sn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.92</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O demonstrates that Sn-doped materials of FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O as cathodes for SIBs exhibited a high discharge specific capacity of 115.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and demonstrated a fabulous capacity retention of 82% after 100 cycles at 20 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. By combining theoretical calculations with morphological and structural characterization, it is inferred that Sn doping narrows the bandgap width of the FeF<small><sub>3</sub></small>·0.33H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O material, leading to enhanced conductivity, and enlarges the unit cell size, facilitating the improvement of the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion rate.</span>
